As the German, French and UK Governments, together with a number of other Member States, clearly stated their concern at the implications of the imminent abolition of the EU duty‐free trade at the recent ECOFIN meeting, particularly in relation to the social and economic problems that are already becoming apparent in the recent announcement of job losses in a number of Member States, would the Presidency not concede that, with employment being today's priority for the EU, all Member States should be prepared to review a decision taken seven years ago and reassess it against today's economic realities?

Joint answer

With regard to the decision taken in 1991 on duty‐free sales for travellers between the Member States, the European Council has asked the Commission and the ECOFIN Council to examine by March 1999 the problems which may arise with regard to employment and, on the basis of proposals from the Commission, to look at how these problems can be dealt with, including the possibility of extending the transitional provisions for a limited period.

Question No 31 by Paul Rübig (H−1121/98)

Subject: Accession strategy for Slovakia

The parliamentary elections held in the Slovak Republic on 25 and 26 September 1998 have brought about a fundamental change in the political situation in that country. In its coalition agreement, the new government has committed itself to speeding up the integration process and, in particular, to fulfilling the Copenhagen criteria as soon as possible. The Slovak legal system is also to be adapted so as to further consolidate the principles of a market economy and democracy.

The efforts of the Slovak people and its government mean that there are now also high expectations of the EU's decision‐makers. In view of the declarations made at the Vienna Summit, what proposals would the Council Presidency like to leave for its successors to develop further?

Answer

The new situation in Slovakia following the parliamentary elections on 25 and 26 September augurs well for its integration into European structures. The way in which the elections were conducted and the high turn‐out demonstrate the Slovak people's clear commitment to the democratisation process.

The Council welcomes the new Slovak government's positive attitude, and in particular its openly stated and resolute willingness to satisfy the Copenhagen criteria at the earliest possible opportunity. Furthermore, at its meeting on 7 December 1998 the Council noted the Commission's view that the new situation in Slovakia following the elections paved the way for negotiations, on condition that the orderly, stable and democratic functioning of its institutions was confirmed. The Commission's conclusions to this effect have been published.

In view of the political priorities identified in the Accession Partnership, the Council would point out the importance of the intensive pre‐accession strategy and would encourage Slovakia to step up its preparations for membership, taking advantage of all available EU assistance and applying the provisions of the Europe Agreement.

Question No 32 by Jonas Sjöstedt (H−1127/98)

Subject: Schengen visa rules

When the Amsterdam Treaty enters into force, the provisions of the Schengen Agreement will become part of EU law. Despite that, important parts of the implementing rules for the Schengen Agreement are not public, including the manual with instructions to embassies and consulates. This manual describes the conditions for issuing visas for the Schengen area. According to reports in the Danish press, which refer to a copy of the manual from December 1994, these instructions contain restrictions on the prospects of anyone unemployed obtaining a Schengen visa. The unemployed are identified as a special risk group for entry. Can the Council confirm that this description is contained in the manual currently applicable to the Schengen Agreement?

Answer

As the Treaty of Amsterdam has not yet come into force, the Schengen Treaty arrangements fall outside the European Union. Legally speaking, the Council is not therefore in a position to give the honourable Member information on rules established under the Schengen arrangements.

However, I would like to add that the Schengen presidency regularly provides the European Parliament with information on the outcome of the deliberations of the Schengen group.

Question No 33 by Bernd Posselt (H−1129/98)

Subject: Integration of Croatia into the EU

In the opinion of the Council Presidency, what concrete conditions must Croatia meet, and within what period, in order to benefit fully from the PHARE programme and to be able to negotiate a trade and cooperation agreement as a preliminary step towards a Europe Agreement?

Answer

In answering the honourable Member's question I would like to emphasise that Croatia must comply with the conditions set down under the regional concept in order to benefit fully from the PHARE programme and to enter into negotiations on a trade and cooperation agreement as a preliminary step towards a Europe agreement.

As I am sure the honourable Member is aware, in April 1997 the Council adopted an overall strategy, known as the regional concept, on relations with the west Balkan countries: Croatia, Bosnia and Herzegovina, the Federal Republic of Yugoslavia, the former Yugoslav Republic of Macedonia and Albania. In an effort to consolidate peace and stability in that region, to assist its economic reconstruction and to promote its European aspirations, it was agreed that the EU would gradually extend its relations with these countries. To this end the EU laid down political and economic conditions to be satisfied as a prerequisite firstly for autonomous trade preferences, then for admission to PHARE and ultimately for treaty relations. The Council regularly monitors progress in complying with these conditions and most recently adopted conclusions on this subject on 9 November 1998.

Question No 34 by Marie‐Paule Kestelijn‐Sierens (H−1134/98)

Subject: Scenario for the introduction of the euro

Articles 10 and 11 of Regulation 974/98 of 3 May 1998(1) stipulate that the ECB and the Central Banks of participating Member States shall put euro banknotes and coins into circulation as from 1 January 2002. Does the Council consider that making supplies available to the consumer in order to mount a big‐bang scenario before the official introduction date is compatible with the provisions of that regulation? Does it not, moreover, think that divergent introduction scenarios in the participating countries could create extra problems with the changeover for the Member States, and does it not consequently consider that it would be advisable for Member States to consult on this aspect by way of a special Council meeting?

Answer

According to Articles 10 and 11 of Council Regulation 974/98 as from 1 January 2002 euro banknotes will be put into circulation by the ECB and the national central banks as well as euro coins by participating Member States. Those notes and coins will have legal tender status from that date.

Furthermore, Regulation 974/98 foresees a period of maximum 6 months (which can be shortened by national law) ending at the latest on 1 July 2002, during which banknotes and coins denominated in national currency conserve their legal tender status.

The Council is therefore of the opinion that Regulation 974/98 permits a smooth and gradual introduction of the euro whereby euro banknotes and coins will be the only such means of payment having legal tender status after an appropriate phasing‐in period of maximum 6 months.

Question No 35 by José Salafranca Sánchez‐Neyra (H−1136/98)

Subject: Calling the San José Conference

In view of the appalling consequences of Hurricane Mitch in Central America, and the European Parliament's November part‐session debate on the situation there and what action the European Union should take, does the Presidency‐in‐Office of the Council intend to activate political dialogue and call an extraordinary meeting of the San José Ministerial Conference? If so, does the Presidency‐in‐Office of the Council intend to do so as a matter of the greatest urgency, given how serious the situation is?

Answer

The honourable Member's attention is drawn to the conclusions of the Council of 30 November 1998 according to which:

The Council welcomed the Commission's initiative to prepare an Action Plan on medium‐ and long‐term reconstruction efforts in Central America which should be submitted urgently to the Council and should be discussed in the next few months at a ministerial meeting with the San José Group of countries.

The date has not yet been fixed.

Question No 36 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H−1139/98)

Subject: Deteriorating social situation in Algeria

How will the Union help to prevent the social situation in Algeria from deteriorating to the point of explosion?

Answer

The EU has repeatedly and unreservedly condemned the acts of terrorism which the Algerian people have suffered so long and which have had an adverse effect on the country's economic and social situation. The EU has also stated its commitment to a political dialogue with Algeria and remained supportive of the government's efforts to further consolidate democracy in the country and to protect their citizens from terrorism, within the rule of law and consistent with human rights. The Council will employ all means at its disposal, of a formal or informal nature, to invite the Algerian authorities to abide by the rules of law and justice in order to prevent the social situation deteriorating any further.

For the benefit of Algerian people, the European Union recently approved cooperation programmes worth 95 MECU to be implemented under the MEDA regulation. The general objectives of those programmes aim at responding to the need to improve the living conditions of the Algerian population through a more liberal economic policy. Due to the worsening situation in refugee camps, the EU has also granted 10 MECU in humanitarian aid to Sahrawi refugees in October 1998. However, the delivery of aid to the target groups as well as planning and launching of projects, have been delayed and deteriorated due to the difficult political situation in the country.

The Council adopted negotiation directives for an Association Agreement with Algeria on 10 June 1996. The agreement will, among other things, establish economic, financial and technical cooperation with Algeria to help ensure the sustainable economic and social development of the country. Negotiations with Algeria were opened on 4‐5 March 1997 but considerably slowed down. This is due to the fact that Algeria has needed more time for the preparation of the negotiations. The Council follows closely all aspects related to the negotiations.

Algeria is also a partner in the Barcelona Process in which the participants have set themselves several long‐term objectives in the economic and financial field, e.g. improvement of the living conditions of their populations, increase in the employment level and reduction in the development gap in the Euro‐Mediterranean region.

Question No 37 by Liam Hyland (H−1141/98)

Subject: E‐commerce and consumer security

With estimates that the European market for e‐commerce will grow from USD 38.5 million in 1997 to USD 8.07 billion in 2004 and that the Internet market will increase from 9.9 million to 44.9 million subscribers over the same period, what assurances can the Council give that financial transactions conducted via e‐commerce will be secure, trustworthy and good for consumers and businesses alike?

Answer

The Council is fully aware of the issues relating to security and trust in electronic commerce and would like to remind the honourable Member that already in 1997 it adopted conclusions both on ‘Electronic Commerce' (13 November) and ‘Digital Signatures and Encryption' (1 December), inviting in particular the Commission to submit a European Parliament and Council directive on digital signatures as soon as possible.

This directive was presented in May 1998. Given the importance of this proposal, the Council had a first in‐depth debate on 27 November 1998 on this dossier and once it has received the opinion of the European Parliament, the Council will endeavour to adopt the common position as soon as possible. Therefore the Council would appreciate the opinion of your institution quickly.

The Commission has just submitted a proposal on specific legal aspects of electronic commerce in the internal market. This proposal is also a priority for the Council and a brief exchange of views was held when it was presented by Commissioner Monti on 7 December 1998. This proposal aims to facilitate the development of electronic commerce in the single market, whist at the same time ensuring a high level of consumer protection.

Question No 38 by James Fitzsimons (H−1143/98)

Subject: Creation of parks and gardens in urban areas

Has the Council considered any new initiatives with a view to supporting, financially or otherwise, the preservation of parks and gardens in cities and small rural towns, and will it call on the Commission to study what new measures might be proposed by local authorities with a view to providing further parks and gardens?

Answer

The honourable Member will certainly be aware that the Commission has submitted proposals to the Council for the reform of the Structural Funds for the period 2000 to 2006.

In these proposals, the Commission makes provision for structural assistance for urban areas in decline and rural areas.

The Council has not yet completed its consideration of the Commission's proposals and consequently has not yet adopted a position which might provide an answer to the honourable Member's question.

Question No 39 by Brian Crowley (H−1145/98)

Subject: Follow‐up to the June 1998 UN Drugs Conference

Will the Council outline what action has been undertaken or is being considered with a view to the rapid implementation of the conclusions of the June 1998 UN Drugs Conference and, in particular, the Annex to the Political Declaration which commits the United Nations Member States to reduce the illicit supply of and demand for drugs and, among other objectives, reaffirms the need for eradication programmes (cultivation) and undertakes to ensure that men, women and children benefit from their proposed actions?

Answer

The implementation of commitments endorsed at the Special Session of the United Nations General Assembly held from 9/10 June 1998 in New York has been a priority of the Austrian presidency.

The executive director of UNDCP, Mr Arlacchi, has presented on 24 September 1998 the views of his organisation on this matter to the Horizontal Drugs Group.

The integrated balanced approach towards the drugs problem endorsed in New York gives a specific place to demand reduction efforts. Council bodies have identified possibilities for enhancement of cooperation in the demand reduction area notably in the fields of approximation of practices. A key role in accumulating know‐how and promoting activities in the field of drug demand reduction is to be given to the European Monitoring Centre on Drugs and Drug Addiction.

Supply reduction, including eradication is a major component of the Action plan on Latin America that is being drawn up on the basis of 66 Recommendations of the Experts mission to Latin America in 1996.

In this context a successful consultative meeting on Peru, co‐sponsored by the European Commission took place on 10 November 1998 in Brussels.

The third high‐level meeting of drugs experts of Member States of the Andean Community and the European Union in Cartagena on 25/26 November 1998 has allowed for an in‐depth exchange of views notably on alternative development strategies.

The Austrian presidency has started to work on the definition of a post−1999 strategy. Work in this field will be carried on under the incoming German presidency. In the elaboration of this strategy a synergy with the follow‐up to the UNGASS process will be assured. The Member States of the European Union have been coordinating their positions in view of the first formal UNGASS follow‐up meeting of UNDCP on 14/15 December 1998 in Vienna on demand reduction. In all post‐UNGASS activities appropriate attention will be given that men, women and children should benefit from the proposed actions.

Question No 40 by Niall Andrews (H−1147/98)

Subject: Cuba

Cuba's Communist Politburo has apparently decided to restore Christmas on a permanent basis. How does the President‐in‐Office of the Council view this development, and how does he see EU‐Cuba relations developing in the New Year and into the Millennium?

Answer

The Council is not in a position to comment on the decision of Cuba's Communist Politburo to which the honourable Member refers in his question.

However, the Council took note of the evaluation and the proposals for further implementation of the Common Position on Cuba. The Council noted that in the last six months the European Union has intensified its dialogue with the Cuban authorities and all sectors of Cuban society, in particular regarding human rights. The Council reconfirmed the strong desire of the European Union to act as Cuba's partner with a view to the progressive and irreversible opening of the Cuban economy. It nevertheless considered that full cooperation with Cuba will depend on an improvement of the situation regarding human rights and fundamental freedoms.

The Council reiterated that the objective of the European Union towards Cuba remained the encouragement of a process of peaceful transition to pluralist democracy, the respect for human rights and fundamental freedoms, as well as sustainable economic recovery and improvement in the living standards of the Cuban people.

The Council therefore reconfirms the position of the Union as set out on 2 December 1996, adopts the Presidency's recommendations on the evaluation and implementation of the Common Position and decides to evaluate the position after a further six months.

Question No 41 by Pat Gallagher (H−1149/98)

Subject: Tackling AIDS and the poverty‐AIDS link

Wednesday, 2 December 1998 was World Aids Day.

Will the Council say what initiatives it undertook under the Austrian Presidency to tackle this appalling disease not only within the EU but also in Africa, where poverty and AIDS are directly linked?

Answer

The five‐year programme of Community action on the prevention of AIDS and certain other communicable diseases within the framework for action in the field of public health (1996‐2000), which was adopted on 29 March 1996, is currently being implemented. This programme brings together the various measures in the field of prevention at Community level.

With regard to action at international level, it should be stressed that at the meeting of the ‘Health Council' of 12 November 1998, under the agenda item ‘Any other business', the French delegation submitted a note concerning a French initiative to set up a solidarity fund for international treatment, the main aim of which is to improve access to treatment, including anti‐retroviral treatments, for persons with HIV/AIDS in the developing countries, and appealed to the Member States to support this initiative.

Question No 42 by Mark Killilea (H−1151/98)

Subject: Employment policy

Will the President‐in‐Office of the Council outline the initiatives undertaken during the Austrian Presidency with a view to tackling unemployment, including long‐term unemployment and unemployment among young people, and how does it assess the success of its policies at EU level in this regard?

Answer

The honourable Member is undoubtedly aware that employment was a top priority for the Austrian Presidency.

The joint ECOFIN/Employment and Social Affairs Council of 1 December 1998 adopted the joint report on employment for 1998 and the guidelines for the Member States' employment policies for 1999. These documents will be submitted to the European Council in Vienna. The guidelines will then be formally adopted in the form of a resolution, in accordance with the procedure followed in 1998. They will be published in the Official Journal. Needless to say, youth employment and long‐term unemployment were among the main elements taken into consideration in the guidelines, as was the case in 1998 (see Council resolution of 15 December 1997, OJ C 30, 28.1.1998). Two of the guidelines are devoted specifically to these issues and the Member States have made considerable efforts in their national employment plans to implement them. These guidelines have remained unchanged, thereby ensuring continuity in the measures taken in these fields.

Question No 43 by Per Gahrton (H−1154/98)

Subject: Schengen and the applicant countries

In the speech he delivered at the November 1998 part‐session in Strasbourg, the President of Poland, Alexander Kwasniewski, expressed his concern that the Schengen rules would make it difficult for people from Poland's eastern and southern neighbours, e.g. Ukraine, to visit Poland. He called for Poland to be allowed to take responsibility itself for maintaining ‘friendly' borders with neighbouring countries which have not joined the EU yet, or do not yet wish to join, or are still unable to join. Similarly, Hungary has warned that the Schengen rules may create new barriers between it and the large Hungarian minorities in countries such as Romania and Slovakia. There is also uneasiness in the Czech Republic about being forced to tighten up controls on its border with Slovakia, with which until not so long ago it had no border at all. There is concern in the Baltic States that it will be even harder to travel between them than it was during the Soviet era. Slovenia, too, is unhappy about having to introduce very tough border controls vis‐à‐vis other former Yugoslav republics.

What is the Council's position on the concerns that exist in several applicant countries about being forced to erect new barriers on their borders with old neighbours who, for the time being at any rate, will remain outside the EU? Is it prepared for instance to grant derogations from the Schengen rules so as to allow new EU Member States to use their discretion in devising ways of maintaining ‘friendly' borders with neighbouring countries who will be remain outside the EU for the time being?

Answer

The integration of the Schengen acquis has been established by the Treaty of Amsterdam. Since up to now the Treaty of Amsterdam has not entered into force and the Schengen provisions have not yet been integrated into the framework of the European Union, the Council is not in a position to comment on the matters referred to in the honourable Member's question.

Question No 44 by Hans Lindqvist (H−1168/98)

Subject: The consequences of the EU's banana policy

US trade authorities have said that, unless the EU and USA reach an agreement on bananas imported into the EU, they will retaliate by imposing special rates of customs duty on certain categories of goods which the USA imports from the EU. This would hit a number of Swedish firms hard. If retaliatory tariffs are introduced, hundreds of Swedes could lose their jobs because of what is known as the banana war. The import duties introduced by the EU to curb imports of dollar bananas has also led to huge increases in the price of bananas in Swedish shops, as Swedish consumers happen to prefer dollar bananas. Is it not time for the EU to reconsider its policy on bananas at once and also to take account of its policy's consequences for firms, for instance in Sweden, that will now be badly affected by US retaliatory tariffs? And is it not high time that EU bananas were made to compete on the free market without tariff protection?

Answer

I would first like to point out that in adopting the new common organisation of the market in bananas in July of this year, the Council had in mind the three following basic objectives:

‐ strict observance of the obligations entered into in the framework of the WTO and of the conclusions of the Standing Appellate Body, as required in the circumstances;

‐ equally strict observance of the obligations flowing from other agreements entered into by the Community, notably the Lomé Convention;

‐ consideration of the legitimate interests of both consumers and producers in the Community.

I can assure you that the Council believes that the regime adopted following the opinion of the European Parliament complies with these three objectives in every respect, and that in particular it takes due account of the interests of the Community's trading partners. The regime contains none of the provisions of the previous regime which the Appellate Body had described as being in clear contravention of the WTO's own rules.

Of course, opinions differ as to whether the banana regime amended in this way is in accordance with the conclusions of the WTO Appellate Body. As far as our trading partners are concerned, it goes without saying that any dispute must be resolved in accordance with the WTO's multilateral system for the settlement of disputes. The Council would like to draw attention in particular to Article 23(2)(b) of the Understanding on Rules and Procedures Governing the Settlement of Disputes, according to which any unilateral measure relating to observance of the Agreement establishing the WTO is forbidden.

Concerning the implementation of the new market organisation, especially with regard to its impact on consumers, the Council notes that all such questions, particularly in connection with this specific aspect, come under Regulation (EC) No 2362/98, which was adopted by the Commission on 28 October 1998 in accordance with the procedure of the Management Committee for Bananas. This regulation lays down the provisions of the import regime due to apply as from 1 January of next year. It would therefore appear premature to make observations at this point about the market situation within the Member States.

The Council reaffirms that in implementing this regime its intention has been to promote healthy competition which is first and foremost in the interest of consumers.

Question No 45 by Christine Oddy (H−1169/98)

Subject: Aid to help those affected by Hurricane Mitch

What is the latest position in relation to the devastation wrought by Hurricane Mitch in Central America? How much European Union aid has been delivered, and what are the most urgent needs of the people now?

Answer

The latest position adopted by the Council on the matter referred to by the honourable Member is its conclusions of 30 November.

In these conclusions, the Council emphasised the grave humanitarian and economic situation in Central America following hurricane MITCH. It welcomed the rapid response to date by the Commission and Member States to the consequence of this catastrophe for the region by the provision of humanitarian aid.

Furthermore, the Council stressed the urgent need that now exists for rehabilitation in the Central American region, and the need to address the long term social, economic and environmental issues involved. It welcomed the Commission's initiative to prepare an Action Plan on medium‐ and long‐term reconstruction efforts in Central America. This plan should be submitted urgently to the Council and also should be discussed in the next few months at a ministerial meeting with the San José Group of countries.

The Council also stressed the grave debt situation of the countries affected and the need for the international community to address, as a matter of urgency, their short‐ and long‐term debt situation. It also recalled its conclusions on 23 November 1998 and noted that it would come back to this issue.

Finally, the Council considered it necessary to ensure maximum effectiveness in addressing the consequences of the hurricane and, in this context, emphasised the need for all bilateral and multilateral donors, including the Community and the Member States, to pursue a coordinated and coherent approach.

Exact information on the financial aid provided to the victims of the hurricane is not at the disposal of the Council. At the Council meeting on 9 November, a rough estimate of nearly 100 MECU was made comprising both EC budget and Member States contributions. Since then, more aid has been decided and still more is being studied for the medium term. Furthermore, debt moratoria and relief has been decided by more Member States.

The Council has received a report by Commissioner Bonino of her visit to Central America. A Consultative Group of donors is meeting in Washington on 10/11 December in order to coordinate aid for reconstruction in the region.

II. Questions to the Commission

 

Question No 55 by Astrid Thors (H−1118/98)

Subject: Use of legislation adopted by the Council of Europe in issues coming under the third pillar

The screening process is an important part of the enlargement process. Within the EU, interinstitutional structures have been established also for third‐pillar screening. In the third pillar, much of the acquis communautaire consists of conventions, joint actions and other forms of EU soft law. As is well known, with the consent of all EU governments, as members of the Council of Europe, the Council of Europe has adopted relevant Conventions and Recommendations in, or relating to, the field of immigration and asylum, some of which the EU is also using as a basis for its own legislation and soft law. These COE Conventions and Recommendations are more precise than the joint actions, and they pay due attention to human rights. The COE Conventions and Recommendations have already successfully contributed to the development of human rights standards and legislative/administrative infrastructure concerning asylum and immigration in most of the associated States.

How is the Commission going to use the body of legislation adopted by the Council of Europe in issues coming under the third pillar when conducting the screening process, and would it be willing to invite experts from the Council of Europe to participate in the screening?

Answer

The honourable Member is right to point out that Conventions and other instruments negotiated by the Council of Europe constitute a significant element of the Justice and Home Affairs acquis . The Commission is aware that, as members of the Council of Europe, the candidate countries have already acceded, or are in the process of acceding to many of these Conventions. The Commission monitors the state of the candidates' accession to these Conventions, notably in the context of the ongoing screening process. To date this has, so far as Justice and Home Affairs are concerned, included multi‐lateral sessions with Bulgaria, Latvia, Lithuania, Romania and Slovakia. In future, there will be bilateral sessions, initially with those countries with whom accession negotiations have been opened (Cyprus, the Czech Republic, Estonia, Hungary, Poland and Slovenia). These bilateral sessions in particular will provide an opportunity for the Commission to explore to what extent the candidate countries have implemented those instruments negotiated in other international fora.

In conducting the initial part of the screening process, the Commission has concentrated primarily on the EU's own acquis , rather than seeking to explain to the candidate countries the content of Conventions adopted in other bodies, such as the Council of Europe and the UN, of which they are already members. The Commission is however willing and able to answer questions on the European Union's attitude towards these Conventions, if the candidate countries ask them in the course of the screening process.

For that reason, the Commission does not intend to invite Council of Europe experts to take part in the screening sessions, particularly since the Council of Europe has its own arrangements by which the candidate countries can accede to its Conventions.

Question No 56 by Mihail Papayannakis (H−1124/98)

Subject: Community programmes for Kurdish refugees

According to Mrs Gradin's answer to my previous question on the Kurdish problem (P−0024/98)(2) , the Commission has implemented budget heading B5‐803 to improve the reception for asylum‐seekers and displaced persons on the territory of the Member States and budget heading B3‐4143 aimed at the integration of refugees. Will the Commission say how many programmes the Greek Government has submitted for the integration of Kurdish refugees in Greece and which programmes these are? What results have the programmes achieved?

Answer

In 1998, the Commission issued guidelines for implementing budget line B5‐803, which is concerned with improving reception conditions for asylum seekers in the European Union. The Commission's guidelines stated that it would ‘particularly welcome project proposals which are designed to improve reception and facilitate access to asylum procedures in the country of first arrival in the European Union for asylum seekers and displaced persons from Iraq and the neighbouring region, ' in accordance with point 8 of the EU action plan on the influx of migrants from Iraq and the neighbouring region.

Of the total 3.75 million ECU available under budget line B5‐803, almost 600 000 ECU was allocated to two Greek projects, and approximately 870 000 ECU was allocated to two Italian projects. The two main countries of first arrival for persons from Iraq and the neighbouring region therefore received a substantial proportion of the funds available.

With respect to budget line B3‐4113 on the integration of refugees, under which 10 million ECU was available in 1998, 10 proposals relating to the integration of refugees in Greece were received, and a total of 432 000 ECU was allocated to 4 projects which were supported. All the projects are designed to help any refugee who has settled in the area concerned, and none of them target specific ethnic groups. No proposals were received with Kurdish refugees as the specific target group.

Both the budget line on the reception of asylum seekers and the budget line on the integration of refugees funding were new in 1997. Most projects are for one year, and the 1997 projects began at the end of that year. Final results of projects funded both in 1997 and 1998 are therefore not yet available.

Question No 57 by Bernd Posselt (H−1130/98)

Subject: Justice and Home Affairs and the applicant countries

What is the current state of play as regards cooperation with the applicant countries and other Central and Eastern European countries in the fields of Justice and Home Affairs? After the elections held there on 27 September 1998, has Slovakia now been included in such cooperation, and is there any cooperation with Croatia on internal security issues?

Answer

The honourable Member has asked a question concerning the cooperation with the candidate countries in the area of Justice and Home Affairs.

The enlargement process is now well under way. The European Council in Vienna last weekend recognised the progress that has been achieved so far. In parallel to the enlargement process, a substantial pre‐accession strategy has been established which covers all areas of Union policies. The aim is to prepare candidate countries well in advance for membership.

The Association Agreements remain the basis for this cooperation. These Agreements include cooperation in the area of Justice and Home Affairs, and the technical assistance offered in this context is substantial.

The Essen European Council in December 1994 extended PHARE assistance to Justice and Home Affairs. The financial commitments in this field now amounts to ECU 113 m.

In January next year the Commission will put a proposal to the Council to open up the Title VI programmes to candidate countries. This will allow these countries to propose projects themselves under multi‐annual programmes such as GROTIUS, STOP, OISIN, ODYSSEUS and FALCONE.

Justice and Home Affairs also features in the screening process, which is the very first step in the enlargement process. This screening has already started with some of the candidate countries and will be continued and intensified in February and March of next year.

The honourable Member refers in particular to cooperation with Slovakia and Croatia in this area. With regard to Slovakia, the European Union has already made clear its favourable reaction to the result of the elections held on 27 September. This is reflected in the Commission's Regular Report on Slovakia's progress towards accession.

Provided that this positive development continues, this would facilitate a decision to open up negotiations with Slovakia, and indeed some other candidate countries – in particular to two Baltic States, Latvia and Lithuania. In the meantime Slovakia is participating fully in the pre‐accession strategy also in Justice and Home Affairs.

As regards Croatia, the Commission would refer to the comprehensive strategy the Council adopted in 1997 for the relations with the countries in the Western Balkans.

This strategy established political and economic conditions, which have to be fulfilled in order to qualify for PHARE assistance and for establishing contractual relations between the EU and Croatia.

The Council monitors the progress made towards these conditions regularly. The last conclusions on the issue were adopted on 9 November 1998. These conclusions state that the conditions for assistance under the PHARE programme have not yet been fulfilled and negotiations on a cooperation agreement would therefore be premature.

Question No 62 by Gerard Collins (H−1108/98)

Subject: Urban and rural renewal tax breaks

Does the European Commission recognise the importance that government‐supported urban and rural renewal tax breaks play in developing local economies in Ireland and will the European Commission continue to support such initiatives in Ireland in the future and if so can the Commission confirm that it has received applications from the Irish Government this year for a continuation of such tax breaks and give an indication of when it intends to reply to these applications?

Answer

When assessing the compatibility with the common market of urban and rural renewal tax reliefs, the Commission will evaluate and take into account, within the possibilities allowed by the regional aid rules, arguments supporting the importance of such reliefs for local economies in Ireland.

These reliefs contain both investment and operating aid. As a general rule, investment aid is allowed in all eligible regions, provided that it complies with the regional aid rules. Operating aid, on the contrary, can be allowed exceptionally and for well‐defined periods, only in regions eligible for regional aid under Article 92 (3) (a) of the EC Treaty, namely those which have a GDP per capita lower than 75 % of the Community average. Moreover, the approval of operating aid is subject to strict conditions, given the fact that it is not linked to an investment, but aims at reducing costs which should be normally borne by the companies themselves.

In examining the urban and rural tax breaks for the future, and considering their long‐lasting effect, the Commission must take into account the present socio‐economic situation of Ireland and the fact that the present regional aid map expires on 31.12.99.

The Commission can confirm that it has received, on 17 September 1998, two notifications concerning the extension of the urban and rural renewal schemes which, however, needed to be completely through the provision of additional information by the Irish authorities. A decision on these schemes will be taken within two months from the receipt of complete information. In the course of this year the Commission has also examined the extensions (notified and not) of the urban renewal scheme, which have been made in the period after the initial approval of that scheme by the Commission, dating more than ten years ago, namely in 1986. The respective decisions will be issued very shortly.

Question No 63 by Richard Howitt (H−1156/98)

Subject: Is European competition policy a Titanic failure for life rafts?

For many years now, there have been only three UK manufacturers of life rafts. Each of these manufacturers had approved service agents throughout the country which they used. Following a merger between two of these groups (Beaufort and Dunlop), contracts with all the former servicing companies have now been terminated and have been given to just one company. South Eastern Marine Services in Basildon, Essex, is just one of many of the raft servicers currently experiencing great difficulties in the face of what appears to be unfair competition. The users of these rafts now have only one company to choose from. Does this not contravene the EU competition policy?

Answer

The merger to which the honourable Member refers does not fall within the Commission's jurisdiction. It was examined and cleared under UK competition law and, therefore, is a matter for the British Secretary of State for Trade and Industry.

Question No 64 by Caroline Jackson (H−1056/98)

Subject: EU attitude to Casares (Spain) landfill site

Is the Commission satisfied that the waste treatment plant and landfill site at Casares (which is part‐financed by the EU) is being developed in a lawful manner, given the refusal of the developers to publish their plans, given that a large part of the area to be developed as a landfill site is a designated forest area and that there is no evidence that this designation has been revoked, and given that a local petition against the development has now been brought before the Spanish courts?

Answer

The Commission has already received several submissions, complaints and letters concerning the waste treatment plant at Casares in the western part of the Costa del Sol. As the project is funded under the POMAL Programme (Local Environment Operational Programme) of the ERDF, representatives of the Commission, together with officials from the Spanish Ministry of Finance and Ministry of the Environment, have visited the site in order to take stock of the situation. Following this visit and the additional information provided by the Spanish authorities, it appears that the waste treatment plant to be built at Casares is based on a number of studies and is part of the overall provincial plan for urban waste disposal of the province of Malaga, drawn up as long ago as 1987 and subsequently adapted several times (1992, 1994 and 1997) to take account of technical and legal developments in the field.

From the outset, the plan made provision for a waste treatment plant for municipal areas in the western Costa del Sol. The decision to build the plant at Casares was the result of an environmental impact assessment, which included public consultations, together with preventive and corrective measures in line with the requirements of Directive 85/337/EEC(3) on the evaluation of the impact on the environment of certain public and private projects. The assessment made provision for a monitoring and inspection programme. Casares was chosen from among six possible sites because its location met the necessary conditions as regards ease of access, geological characteristics of the land and distance from the population centres served.

The project secured the approval of the authorities responsible for the environment of the Governing Council of Andalucia and the Provincial Council of Malaga.

In the light of the above and on the basis of information provided to the Commission, both by the parties concerned and the Spanish authorities, no breach of the abovementioned directives can be established in this case.

Question No 65 by Nuala Ahern (H−1058/98)

Subject: Plans to substitute BNFL‐owned plutonium for foreign‐owned plutonium contained in radioactive waste arising from the Sellafield MOX plutonium fuels plant

What communications have the relevant Commission directorates or the EURATOM safeguards agency received over the past four years from either the British Environment Agency or the United Kingdom Government in respect of plans to substitute BNFL‐owned plutonium for non‐UK (foreign) owned plutonium contained in radioactive waste arising from the Sellafield MOX plutonium fuels plant, once permission to operate the plant had been granted by the UK Government?

Answer

The Commission would like to inform the honourable Member of Parliament that reports on nuclear material movements and inventories are received by the Commission pursuant to Chapter VII of the Euratom Treaty and Regulation 3227/76. These reports are received directly from operators as required by those provisions. Those concerning the Sellafield MOX fuel fabrication plant are due to be received (see below) from BNFL, the operator.

The British Environment Agency and the United Kingdom Government do not send reports to the Commission on movements of nuclear materials; in any event, they are not required, under Community law, to do so.

In addition, the Commission has not received any communications regarding substitution of plutonium from the Sellafield MOX plant nor has it any information on this subject. It will be noted, moreover, that this plant is not yet in operation and therefore produces no radioactive waste.

Question No 66 by Anne McIntosh (H−1061/98)

Subject: Duty‐free sales

What measures is the Commission proposing, and what action is it considering taking in conjunction with the Member States, to compensate for the losses incurred following the abolition of duty‐free sales from 1 July 1999?

Answer

The Commission has made available on 20 November 1998 an exhaustive document (SEC(1998) 1994) which lists and illustrates all the instruments already existing at the Community level to deal with specific problems that could arise following the abolition of intra‐community duty‐free sales from 1 July 1999. It is up to the Member States to identify possible needs for action and to set the priorities for the use of such instruments. The Commission pointed out in particular that appropriate measures could be taken in the framework of the Community initiative on small and medium enterprises (SMEs), where an important sum of money is still available for funding in 1999.

The Commission is prepared to examine any request made by a Member State within this framework.

Furthermore, the Commission has taken note of the conclusions of the European Council of Vienna and will examine any new elements therein.

Question No 68 by Mark Watts (H−1153/98)

Answer

In the last ECOFIN meeting of 1 December 1998 the point of ‘duty‐free' was raised, and there was no unanimity, nor even a majority, in favour of a modification of the Council Directives EEC No 77/388 (VAT) and EEC 92/12 (excise). Question No 70 by Ludivina García Arias (H−1070/98)

Subject: Cost to the Spanish electricity sector of undergoing a transition to a competitive environment

The Spanish Government has recently concluded an agreement with the electricity sector whereby over 1 billion pesetas of what is described as the ‘cost of transition to a competitive environment' are to be raised through an issue of bonds and loans, which will allow the electricity companies immediate access to the amount in question.

In view of the fact that, in Spain, four companies control 90 % of the market and declare handsome profits and dividends each year, does the Commission consider the amount of funding allocated to the Spanish electricity sector to be compatible with the principle of free competition?

Question No 71 by Manuel Medina Ortega (H−1071/98)

Subject: Cost to the Spanish electricity sector of undergoing a transition to a competitive environment

Under Spain's Law No. 54/97 it is recognised that the country's electricity sector will have to raise approximately 2 billion pesetas in the course of the ten years preceding the total liberalisation of the sector in order to cover the ‘cost of transition to a competitive environment'.

Does the Commission not think that, if some of this money is invested in the construction of new combined heat and power stations over a three‐ or four‐year period, this would constitute concealed state aid which could distort free competition by barring the entry of other groups which could provide greater competition for the benefit of consumers?

Question No 72 by Manuela Frutos Gama (H−1072/98)

Subject: Definition in the European Union of the cost of transition to a competitive environment in the electricity sector

In the 3 November 1998 edition of the El País newspaper a senior spokesman of the Spanish electricity sector expressed the view that the cost of transition to a competitive environment (which in the case of Spain is estimated at approximately 2 billion pesetas) should be borne by the state and that the funding to be provided for the purpose should be regarded as a ‘legitimate acquired right' of the electricity companies and ‘under no circumstances' as ‘aid which they need in order to survive in a competitive environment', but rather as ‘a means to enable earlier investment to be fully recouped' and ‘the cost of uncertainty' to be eliminated.

Are these views in line with the definition of the ‘cost of transition to a competitive environment' which the Commission is currently drawing up?

Joint answer

The compensation payments which are referred to as CTC ('costes de transición a la competencia') have been notified by the Spanish government according to article 24 of the directive 96/92/EC concerning common rules for the internal market in electricity.

Article 24 provides for the possibility of transitional regimes of limited duration which may be granted by the Commission with regard to commitments and guarantees of operation given before the entry into force of directive 96/92/EC, which may not be honoured on account of the provisions of this Directive. The transitional regime may cover derogations from Chapter IV, VI and VII of this Directive. Insofar as the measures notified for such transitional regimes do not represent derogations from the abovementioned chapters of the Directive, but rather represent payments to electricity companies, the Commission considers that those payments should be examined on the basis of Article 90, 92 and 93 of the EC Treaty.

The Commission is presently examining the Spanish case in this light, along with the schemes notified by other Member States, and is treating this matter as a priority issue. With regard to the specific part of the questions of Ms Garcia Arias, Mr Manuel Medina Ortega and Ms Frutos Gama, it is not possible, at present, to give any indication as to the substance of the case.

Question No 73 by Mary Banotti (H−1080/98)

Subject: Duty‐free allowances – Commissioners

Can the Commission confirm that Members of the Commission are entitled to generous duty‐free allowances of alcohol, cigarettes and petrol?

Are they to be phased out next year along with duty‐free sales?

Can the Commission indicate what these allowances are?

Answer

Belgium accords the 20 members of the Commission the same status as diplomats and they therefore enjoy certain financial benefits granted to all serving diplomats in this Member State and to those persons accorded the same status.

One of these benefits is the VAT‐free purchase of goods for personal use, provided that the total amount is no more than BFR 10 000 before VAT and subject to the following annual allowances for individual products (1998 figures):

Alcoholic drinks: 90 litres
Tobacco: 20 000 cigarettes
Wines: 450 litres
Fuel: first car: 5 000 litres

The conditions of purchase are established each year by the Belgian Ministry of Finance.

The benefits enjoyed by persons having diplomatic status in a country other than their country of origin are generally recognised by all countries. They have their origins in the customs observed in international relations and, at Community level, are covered by the specific provisions of the directives in force on VAT and excise duties(5) and the regulation on customs duties(6) . As they stand at present, these provisions do not include any expiry date.

The duty‐free sales for intra‐Community travellers on ferries and aeroplanes, to which the honourable Member is probably referring, were introduced on a temporary basis by Directive 91/680/EEC, amending Council Directives 77/388 (VAT) and 92/12 (excise duties) and are due to expire on 30 June 1999.

Question No 74 by Hadar Cars (H−1081/98)

Subject: Enlargement

In the Commission's report of July 1997 on a stronger and wider Union, it was stated that none of the applicant countries completely fulfilled the criterion concerning the existence of a functioning market economy. However, the Commission did not regard this as an obstacle to approving the opening of membership negotiations with six countries.

In the conclusions which the Commission has recently presented (composite paper) on enlargement, it is stressed that the functioning market economy criterion must be fulfilled before any country (besides the six selected) can be deemed to meet the requirements for the opening of membership negotiations.

Why has the Commission tightened up these preconditions, and does it have the Council's backing for this more stringent approach?

Answer

In its opinions of July 1997, the Commission examined the accession applications from the applicant countries of Central and Eastern European in the light of the criteria established by the Copenhagen European Council in 1993, which state, in particular, that accession to the Union requires:

‐ the existence of a viable market economy;

‐ the capacity to cope with the pressure of competition and market forces within the Union.

The criterion concerning the market economy must be fulfilled immediately, while the criterion concerning the pressure of competition applies to the medium term.

The countries with whom the Commission recommended opening accession negotiations in 1997, that is, Hungary, Poland, the Czech Republic, Estonia and Slovenia(7) , were all considered to have a viable market economy, capable of coping with the pressure of competition and market forces within the Union in the medium term.

In its composite paper and its regular reports on the progress made by the applicant countries towards accession, the Commission applied the same methodology as that used in its opinions. No other applicant country from Central Europe was considered as having a viable market economy at this time and, therefore, the Commission was unable to recommend the opening of accession negotiations with other applicant countries.

Question No 75 by Hilde Hawlicek (H−1085/98)

Subject: Steps to implement the Council guidelines of March 1998

The European Council of Ministers responsible for development cooperation is to discuss ‘operational coordination' as one of their key topics on 30 November 1998. The efficiency of EU development cooperation depends to a very great extent on this instrument of on‐the‐spot coordination.

What specific steps are currently being discussed in the Commission with a view to implementing the Council guidelines of March 1998?

Answer

During 1998, the Commission took a number of specific measures in order to implement the Council guidelines for strengthening the operational coordination between the Community and the Member States in the field of development cooperation.

Since 25 March, these guidelines have been distributed to all the Commission delegations in developing countries (Latin America, Asia, the Mediterranean, Africa, the Caribbean and the Pacific), along with specific instructions for their implementation. This point was discussed with the heads of the delegations at their last meeting in Brussels at the beginning of September.

The information available to us shows that coordination mechanisms now exist in almost all developing countries, both between Member States and the Commission, and also with other groups that provide support.

The Council of Ministers of 30 November noted the initial progress made and invited the Member States and the Commission to continue their efforts in a pragmatic and pro‐active manner, notably with the aim of enabling the beneficiary government to fully participate in strengthened coordination. At the end of 1999, the Commission and the Member States will present a joint report to the Council setting out the progress made in this area.

On the initiative of the Commission, the director‐generals for development from the Member States and from the Commission have set up a working group on this issue. They met in Brussels at the beginning of the year, and in Vienna on 19 October, in order to discuss the implementation and monitoring methods. They have agreed on a work programme with four main aims:

‐ to introduce a systematic exchange of documentation on strategies between countries and to define together a methodology for achieving those strategies;

‐ to place the emphasis on the practical application of the Council guidelines;

‐ to make specific proposals on the simplification and harmonisation of administrative and financial procedures;

‐ to increase the exchange of documentation on sectorial policies and thematic approaches.

Question No 76 by Arthur Newens (H−1089/98)

Subject: Hurricane Mitch

Is the Commission able to make an up‐to‐date statement on the provision of emergency aid and preparations for the provision of development aid, on relief from the burden of servicing international debt and on the progress made in coping with the damage caused by Hurricane Mitch in each of the countries of Central America which were devastated?

Question No 77 by Christine Oddy (H−1170/98)

Subject: Aid effort and Hurricane Mitch

What is the latest position in relation to the devastation wrought by Hurricane Mitch in Central America? How much European Union aid has been delivered and what are the most urgent needs of the people now?

Joint answer

1. The European Commission contribution is distributed as follows:

a) EMERGENCY AID: As an immediate response to the emergency situation a relief programme amounting to 6.8 MECU was approved by the Commission on 4 November in order to address the needs of the most vulnerable population throughout the region: this aid package included the provision of food parcels, emergency relief items, medical support.

In addition to this the Commission reconverted ongoing disaster preparedness projects in the region worth nearly 3 MECU. Emergency humanitarian aid already mobilised reaches so far 10 MECU.

Following the visit of the member of the Commission responsible for humanitarian aid and on the basis of a comprehensive assessment of needs, the Commission will focus its emergency response on Nicaragua and Honduras, where the emergency is not yet over and humanitarian needs are expected to last for several months to come.

As it is called upon to intensify its presence in the region, the Commission will shortly establish an office in Tegucigalpa and will reinforce its local regional team with a medical experts, as well as a water engineer.

In view of further humanitarian programmes in the short term, the Commission has identified additional resources from available budget allocations (budget line B7‐210) in view of adopting a further relief programme of approximately 8 MECU, to be finalised before the end of December.

The Commission's new funding will mainly focus on water and sanitation, health and temporary shelter. This emergency aid should as far as possible also include a sizeable rehabilitation component, in view of preparing the transition towards more structured rehabilitation, in the spirit of the ‘continuum strategy'.

b) FOOD AID: a 15 MECU package, financed out of Food Security provisions is currently being finalised. This aid programme, to be implemented via NGOs, will include traditional food aid distribution, as well as ‘seeds and tools' programmes, for basic rehabilitation in rural areas.

c) REHABILITATION PROGRAMME: The Commission has submitted to the ALA Committee in December, under an accelerated procedure, a financing proposal for 8.2 MECU (funded from budget line B7‐310) aimed at financing the cost of establishing in the region a support mechanism for the preparation and implementation of a special rehabilitation programme for Central America. The main features of this programme will be defined at a later stage following coordination with main donors.

2. The Community's global response so far (adding up Commission programmes and bilateral Member States pledges) already reached 251 MECU, according to the information provided at the ECOFIN Council on 1 December.

3. As far as debt issues are concerned, for which the Commission has no competence, the honourable Member's attention is drawn to the relevant statement issued by the Council and the Ministers meeting in the Council on the occasion of the ECOFIN meeting on 1 December.

Question No 78 by José Valverde López (H−1091/98)

Subject: Self‐destructing books

Every year, millions of books in our libraries disintegrate into dust. This is a serious case of ‘sick library syndrome': we are producing books by the million which carry within themselves the seeds of their self‐destruction, and this must stop. As a matter of urgency, technical standards must be introduced for permanent paper, and it must be made compulsory to use a symbol informing the public. For over a decade now, the author of this question has been calling on the Commission to take a binding initiative on the matter.

What action has the Commission taken to follow up the recommendations of the meeting of experts on the conservation of acid papers and the use of permanent paper, organised by the Commission and the Danish government and held in The Hague in December 1991?

Answer

The Commission is closely following the problem of the conservation of books and documents through the use of permanent paper from several points of view:

‐ from a cultural point of view, as we are concerned with preserving an extremely rich cultural heritage. To this end, a European directory of non‐acid and permanent printing paper was published in 1994;

‐ from an environmental point of view, as paper production uses many chemicals that affect the environment;

‐ finally, from a technical point of view. Through monitoring the work of the European standardisation bodies, progress has been observed in this respect, and it is possible that the bodies may eventually define a European standard in this field.

Question No 80 by Luciano Vecchi (H−1096/98)

Subject: Boycott of Italian products in Turkey

Over the last few days a broadly based campaign has been under way in Turkey to drum up support for a boycott of Italian products and interests, which have acquired a prominent place on the Turkish market.

As well as damaging political relations, the campaign, which is being openly fomented by the Turkish authorities, is jeopardising the legitimate interests of hundreds of Italian firms and could lead to adverse consequences for employment.

What steps has the Commission taken or will it take to impress upon the Turkish authorities that the obligations which they have entered into, both under international trade agreements and under the Customs Union Agreement with the Community, prohibit any action aimed at discriminating against the legitimate economic interests of Italian and European firms?

What steps has it taken or will it take to make good the harm which the Turkish authorities have inflicted on the legitimate rights and interests of Italian firms?
