3. Nevertheless, certain practices, notably those being used by public companies, could be found to be in contradiction of Turkey"s obligations under the EC‐Turkey agreements and within the WTO.

4. The Commission is therefore examining these practices in detail and is prepared to act using the means at its disposal if a violation of the EC‐Turkey agreements is discovered.

Question No 81 by Ulla Sandbæk (H−1097/98)

Subject: Schengen

Under the Schengen Convention, a person may be registered in the SIS as an ‘undesirable alien' simply because of his political activities, if one Member State so requests. That person may then be denied entry at any other Member State's borders because he is deemed to be an undesirable in just one country. Last year, Stephanie Mills, a New Zealander and Greenpeace activist, was stopped at Amsterdam airport because France had registered her in the SIS as being an undesirable alien in France.

It will be recalled that the Treaty of Amsterdam integrates the Schengen acquis into the framework of the European Union, so that this form of political registration will become part of European law if the Treaty of Amsterdam enters into force. It should also be noted that Article 13 of the Treaty of Amsterdam, unlike international conventions on basic rights, does not specifically rule out discrimination on the basis of political convictions.

Is the Commission aware of the fact that people, simply because they are politically active and are deemed to be undesirables in one Member State, may be denied entry to all other Member States, and of the consequences this has for the Union's own citizens and their right to move freely? What steps will it take to ensure that registrations do not violate basic rights and freedoms?

Answer

One of the fundamental principles of an area without borders is that a person who is legally present in the territory of one Member State has the right to move around in that entire area. On the other hand, access to the whole of a territory that is an area without borders should, in principle, be denied to a person who cannot be admitted into a Member State that is part of it. An area without internal borders must be an area of both freedom and security.

The Schengen Convention applied that principle:

‐ with regard to the ‘freedom" aspect, the Schengen Convention gives a person who is legally present in the territory of a Schengen state the right to move about in the entire Schengen area, due, in particular, to the principle of mutual recognition of visas (provided that the visa granted by a Member State allows travel in all of the Schengen states) and to the principle of equivalence between a residence permit and a visa (which allows a third country national legally residing in a Member State to enter the territory of other Member States without being subject to the visa obligation);

‐ with regard to the ‘security" aspect, the Schengen Convention includes, in the form of the Schengen Information System, a list of foreigners registered as being inadmissible. The registration of a person on this list is carried out in accordance with the procedures laid down in the legislation of the Member State making the registration: it can be done when the presence of the person in question in the national territory may constitute a threat to public order or national security or safety.

The Member States are naturally obliged to respect fundamental rights when applying the Schengen Convention.

Finally, the Commission cannot agree with the honourable Member"s fears concerning the use of registrations for non‐admission in order to limit the movement of citizens of the Union. In fact, citizens of the Union and members of their families cannot, in any event, be registered on this list. In principle, this also applies to third country nationals who hold a residence permit issued by a Member State from the Schengen area.

Question No 82 by Manuel Escolá Hernando (H−1098/98)

Subject: Death of an EU citizen in Palestine

Uazin Tarifi Marín, aged 15, was shot dead recently by a member of the Palestinian Authority's secret service in the city of Ramalah.

Uazin was apparently on his way to school when he unexpectedly came upon a demonstration in the centre of Ramalah. Then at some point a member of the security service fired a number of shots, killing him.

Given that Uazin's mother, Rosalida Marín, is an EU citizen, and that Uazin himself was of Spanish nationality, was registered as a Spanish citizen at the Spanish Consulate General in Jerusalem and held a valid Spanish passport, has the Commission expressed its condemnation of the killing to the Palestinian authorities, or does it intend to do so? Does it envisage taking action of any kind to establish what actually occurred?

Answer

The Commission deeply regrets the recent tragic accident, which resulted in the death of a fifteen‐year old Spanish national in Ramallah.

The Commission is aware of the need to urgently provide comprehensive training to the Palestinian security services so as to allow them to discharge their duties in an efficient manner which is up to acceptable standards.

In this spirit the EU has undertaken to finance a programme of assistance to the Palestinian Authority security services, which consists of the provision of technical assistance, equipment and training, including on Human Rights, to Palestinian security officers. An EU Security Advisor is in charge of the coordination of the programme with an office in Ramallah. Programme implementation began earlier in 1998.

(NB: EU financing is being made available under a CFSP Joint Action; total cost is ECU 7.6 million) .

The terrible accident in question is likely to be brought up within this framework as a clear example of the type of incident that must be avoided at all costs.

In addition, the matter is also likely to be given proper consideration within the framework of the permanent security committee with the Palestinian Authority, which has been recently established under the umbrella of the EU Special Envoy Ambassador Moratinos.

Question No 83 by Robert Evans (H−1104/98)

Subject: European Union Visitors Programme

Could the Commission confirm that when selecting participants for the EUVP priority is given to those who would not otherwise to able to visit the European Union countries, rather than those who frequently make trips to Europe?

Answer

The Commission can assure the honourable Member that when selecting participants for the European Union Visitors Programme (EUVP) great care is taken to avoid inviting participants with extensive or recent exposure to the European Union. Indeed, at a recent meeting of the EUVP Management Committee, a number of EUVP nominations for 1999 were rejected for this reason.

The EUVP is specifically designed for young/future leaders and priority is given to candidates who are professionally engaged in a field where they have a direct or indirect influence on decision‐making or opinion‐forming, in the hope that their visit to the European Union will affect future policy or attitudes towards the EU.

Question No 84 by James Nicholson (H−1107/98)

Subject: Practical support for the Belfast Agreement

What action has the Commission so far taken in respect of the resolution of the European Parliament of 30 April which calls on the Commission to consider how the Belfast Agreement can be supported in practical terms and in respect of the conclusions of the Cardiff Summit in June which invited the Commission to make proposals accordingly?

Answer

The Commission has responded to Parliament's resolution of 30 April by a proposal to allocate an extra 100 MECU for the PEACE programme for 1999. This amount will be provided by reducing other Community Initiatives across the Community by a similar amount.

The Commission also expects that Northern Ireland will receive a generous allocation of Structural Fund resources between the years 2000 and 2005. A second PEACE programme is not proposed, but similar actions to those funded under PEACE, including the decentralised delivery, should continue in the mainstream funding.

With the Peace Agreement and the North/South Council, Northern Ireland and Ireland are also strong candidates for a significant increase in funding under the next INTERREG Programme.

In addition, the Commission recently invited the Northern Ireland Assembly to a three‐day seminar and fact‐finding conference in Brussels. This visit was made in the context of the Assembly's preparation process prior to taking full responsibility under the agreement; it helped the members to strengthen their knowledge and understanding of European Affairs.

Question No 85 by Peter Truscott (H−1109/98)

Subject: Registration and licensing of hairdressers

Will the Commission consider recommending compulsory EU‐wide registration and licensing for professional hairdressing salons and hairdressers in the European Union? At present, only the UK, Greece and the Republic of Ireland do not license hairdressers, who may be untrained, unqualified and uninsured in those countries. This issue has serious health and safety implications for the citizens of the countries concerned.

Answer

Individual Member States are responsible for regulating particular professions, as regards both access to the profession and the exercise of it.

Nevertheless, the free movement of hairdressers is already guaranteed to a large extent by the transitional directive 82/489/EEC of 19 July 1982 dealing specifically with this profession.

This directive stipulates that when access to or exercise of the profession of hairdresser in a Member State is subject to possession of general, commercial or professional knowledge and skills, the Member State must recognise as sufficient evidence of possession of such knowledge or skills the effective and lawful exercise of these activities in another Member State for a given period.

Furthermore, the Commission has submitted to the Council and the European Parliament a proposal for a directive seeking to extend the scope for freedom of establishment within the internal market for professions covered by transitional directives of the kind relating to hairdressers. Once adopted, this proposal will provide for the recognition of diplomas for those working in the profession and would thus go beyond the provisions of the current directive, which merely provides for access to the profession through automatic recognition of professional experience.

Pending the adoption of this proposal, the recognition of hairdressing diplomas should be assisted by the case‐law of the Court of Justice in connection with the interpretation of Article 52 of the EC Treaty and, specifically, the ‘Vlassopoulou' judgment. Under this ruling, the host Member State is required to examine to what extent the knowledge and skills evidenced by a diploma correspond to those required under its own laws.

The Commission therefore considers that there is no need to propose coordination or harmonisation measures for the hairdressing profession. Similarly, given the existence of national competence in this area and the subsidiarity principle, which must be applied to initiatives at Community level, the Commission does not consider it appropriate to address recommendations to the Member States on this matter.

Finally, the Commission would remind the honourable Member that questions relating to the protection of public health and safety are also, in principle, matters within the competence of the Member States pursuant to Articles 52 and 59 of the EC Treaty and the relevant case‐law of the Court of Justice.

Question No 86 by Lone Dybkjær (H−1110/98)

Subject: Food for special medicinal purposes

Further to the Commission's proposal for a directive on food for special medicinal purposes, Document III/5318/98, and the opinion of the EU's Scientific Committee on Food of 13 December 1996 annexed to document III/5156/97, will the Commission say how often it fails to follow the recommendations it receives from its own scientific committee and on what grounds it has done so in the abovementioned case? How does the Commission imagine that it is possible in practice to ensure that consumers receive the necessary medical advice and that manufacturers comply with their obligation to provide information when it will be possible for food for special medicinal purposes to be sold through general retail outlets, for example?

Answer

The Commission always pays the greatest attention to the opinions of the Scientific Committee for Food (SCF) when formulating proposals on matters likely to have an effect on public health, although these opinions are not binding.

In the case of the SCF opinion concerning foods for special medical purposes, the relevant draft Commission Directive is in line with the great majority of the recommendations of the SCF.

The SCF ‘suggested that the availability or sale of products, likely to be hazardous for persons for whom such products are not intended, should be restricted to health care services or pharmacies'. It is noted that the suggestion covers only a small part of a wide range of medical foods.

The products in question are special products and their labelling, as laid down in the draft directive, should guarantee their appropriate use by the target group of persons. These persons are very sensitised to their needs following medical counsel. Further the labelling shall bear a prominent statement that the product should be used under medical supervision. Manufacturers are obliged to provide the stipulated information.

The Commission therefore has not considered it necessary to lay down any provisions regarding points of sale. It has not done so in any of the other specific directives concerning dietetic foods. Member States may adopt national provisions concerning this specific point provided these provisions are in conformity with the rules of the Treaty.

The SCF also felt that it would be wise to fix upper limits for vitamins and minerals. They suggested that, as a rule, the upper limits should be close to the Population Reference Intakes (PRIs). These suggested levels were not maximum safety levels. The SCF, as written in its report, '..... appreciates however, that this approach is in part arbitrary.....'. In addition the PRIs to which upper limits should be close were those for healthy people.

By definition, as provided for in the draft directive, foods for special medical purposes are intended for patients with a limited, impaired or disturbed capacity to digest, absorb, metabolise or excrete ordinary food or certain nutrients. The particular nutritional needs of these patients are very different. Therefore for some vitamins and minerals upper limits higher than those suggested by the SCF were retained. It should be noted that, in any event, these upper limits are not mandatory but provide a range for the industry to make available products suitable for the needs of a very diverse category of persons which are to be used under medical supervision. It should be further stressed that the retained limits are below generally acceptable safety levels.

The draft directive has been communicated to the European Parliament in accordance with the ‘Modus Vivendi' procedure. The responsible Committee did not lodge any objections.

Question No 87 by Shaun Spiers (H−1114/98)

Subject: European Year of Active Citizenship and the Voluntary Sector

What plans does the Commission have to follow up its excellent Communication on Associations? In particular, what is being done to make it easier for voluntary organisations to apply for EU funds, and what is being done to ensure that 2001 will be designated European Year of Active Citizenship and the Voluntary Sector?

Answer

The Commission has been following up the Communication with a series of national conferences, experts' and inter‐service meetings. In early 1999 the Commission will produce a report on the conclusions from these meetings which will include recommendations for future courses of action. The report will, hopefully, be taken forward as part of a proposed multi‐annual programme in favour of the Social Economy.

The Commission has taken steps to improve access to funding for associations and foundations. The proposals for the regulation of the Structural Funds, including that for the European Social Fund, place great importance on partnerships. The Commission has proposed that, where appropriate, NGOs should form part of the partnership. Furthermore, NGOs will be the recipients of grants made from the 1 % allocated from the funds of each European Social Fund programme. Also, the pilot action, Third System and Employment, the ESF Art 6 pilot projects are currently investigating and promoting the employment potential of third sector organisations in sectors such as social services, environment and culture, and in building local social capital.

No definite decision has yet been made by the Commission as to possible support to any initiative for a European Year of Associations and Active Citizenship 2001 which is proposed by the sector itself. This could be done, for example, in collaboration with the United Nations' International Year of Volunteers which also falls in 2001.

Question No 88 by Paul Lannoye (H−1115/98)

Subject: Referral to the Court of Justice of Italian and Spanish legislation on ‘chocolate'

According to some reports, the European Commission recently considered forwarding a reasoned opinion to Italy and Spain in which it claimed that those countries' legislation – which do not accept the designation ‘chocolate' for products containing vegetable fats other than cocoa butter – was jeopardising the free movement of goods within the EU and was not justified on the grounds of consumer protection. Does the Commission intend to refer this matter to the Court of Justice of the European Communities so that the latter may rule on the validity of the Italian and Spanish legislation?

Since it is aware that the legislative process on the relevant proposal for a directive (COM(95) 0722)(8) is under way in the European Parliament and the Council, does not the Commission consider that, by referring this matter to the Court of Justice, it is encroaching on the preserve of the Community legislature?

Answer

The legislative debate on the proposal for a directive on chocolate, to which the honourable Member is referring, is still under way both in the European Parliament and the Council. Until this proposal is firmly adopted and enters into force, the 1973 Directive (73/241/EEC) on cocoa products and chocolate still applies. The Commission is obliged to ensure that the Member States implement the legislation that is in force and that they respect the principles of the Treaty, particularly with regard to the free movement of goods. In doing this, the Commission is exerting the powers and obligations it is granted by the Treaty, and is not encroaching on the powers of the Community legislature.

Nevertheless, the Commission must point out that the proposal currently being examined by the legislative bodies is not precisely relevant to the facts involved in the infringement procedure concerning Italy and Spain.

In order to continue with the discussions on the proposal for a directive in the various legislative bodies, and in accordance with the commitment it made at the Internal Market Council on 9 November, the Commission is currently considering the various possible solutions for improving the study that has already been completed or for carrying out a new study.

In terms of when the conclusions will be published, although it is not possible at this stage to give an exact date, the Commission is trying to fulfill the commitment it made at the last Internal Market Council as fully as possible.

Question No 91 by Sören Wibe (H−1120/98)

Subject: Durum wheat subsidies

According to a recently published auditors' report, the EU would have saved SEK 27 billion over the last four years if the same subsidies had been granted for the production of durum wheat as for other crops.

What does the Commission intend to do to reduce subsidies for durum wheat production?

Answer

The Commission is naturally aware of the annual report by the Court of Auditors on the 1997 financial year, to which the honourable Member is referring.

In this report, the Court indicates that if the subsidies for durum wheat had been reduced by 30 %, there would have been a total reduction of EUR 3 billion in spending for the 1994‐1997 period.

The honourable Member"s attention is drawn to the answer given by the Commission, which is included in the report. In that answer, the Commission pointed out that a special regime for durum wheat was established by the Council at the beginning of the organisation of the cereals market, due to the lack of alternatives to durum wheat production and to its low yield. This regime was then adapted, notably by the 1992 reform, in which the Council increased subsidies for this sector following the drop in prices.

However, it then decided to reduce it by 4 % from the 1999‐2000 campaign on, which involves applying a system of assured maximum area instead of a system of individual rights, and in which the use of certified seed is obligatory.

This new system was again taken up by the Commission in its Agenda 2000 proposal, which is currently being debated in the Council and Parliament. The Commission does not intend to amend this proposal and will constructively consider any suggestions for improvements to the regime for arable production in general, and for durum wheat in particular.

Question No 92 by Riccardo Garosci (H−1122/98)

Subject: Request for the printing and issuing of the euro 500 note to be delayed for a year

The euro will become part of daily life as from 1 January 2002 when bank‐notes and coins will be issued. During the next three years, when use of the euro will be optional, preparations will be made in the public and private sectors through public information campaigns and business training schemes.

The euro 500 note (worth about ITL 1 million) has too high a face value compared with the notes currently circulating in the 11 euro countries; no existing note in the EMU is worth euro 500. The public (already confronted with 7 new bank‐notes) and business alike are frightened by the value of the euro 500 note and could have difficulty accepting it. In addition there are already rumours of counterfeit euros, a die has already been stolen and there is a danger of receiving. The introduction of the euro must therefore be planned and the public helped to overcome any psychological or practical difficulty that may arise.

Will the Commission therefore delay the printing and issuing of the euro 500 bank‐note by one year, from 1 January 2002 to 1 January 2003?

Answer

In accordance with the Treaty, the preparation and issuing of notes comes under the competence of the ECB. The preparatory work was carried out by the European Monetary Institute (EMI). The decision on the denominations of the notes was taken on 11 September 1998 by the Governing Council of the ECB. It confirmed that there will be seven notes, including a EUR 500 note, in accordance with the EMI recommendation.

The advisability of issuing a EUR 500 note was the subject of analyses carried out by the ECB. The general principal that was followed when defining the unitary values of the euro notes was respect for the current practices and payment habits in the Member States. The ECB"s decision to issue a EUR 500 note is based mainly on the fact that five Member States use notes between EUR 200 and EUR 500. These notes are used as a means of payment in certain transactions and also for storing large amounts of money.

With regard to counterfeiting, the euro notes use the most up‐to‐date protection techniques. Moreover, the ECB has decided to create a technical database and a common investigation centre in order to ensure that the euro notes have a high level of protection against counterfeiting.

The acceptance of the euro notes will be supported by several information campaigns in the Member States, which will be supported by the Commission. All sectors of the public will be targeted. In addition, the Commission is working towards setting up pilot projects for information and for training people who are having difficulties. These information activities will play an essential role in familiarising citizens with the new notes.

Question No 93 by Konstantinos Hatzidakis (H−1125/98)

Subject: Progress with operational programme for education

With regard to the many problems involved in implementing the operational programme ‘Education and initial vocational training' under the Community Support Framework (CSF) for Greece, which was the subject of a previous question of mine to the Commission (E−1803/98), will the Commission say whether the problem of the low take‐up of funds for the programme has been overcome or not and, if not, who is responsible for this state of affairs? What percentage of Community appropriations has been used for the programme as a whole, for each individual sector and, in particular, for the comprehensive secondary school and the 12 new university courses of study? Is there a need for further revision of this programme and, if so, what direction should it take? Finally, does the Commission think that 12 new university courses are sufficient to tackle the problem of unemployment or should there be greater emphasis on linking courses to the needs of industry? Is there not a need to provide more support for postgraduate studies, to promote information technology and to promote measures, in general, which have a greater bearing on employment/ unemployment?

Answer

The take‐up rate for this programme has improved following a number of factors:

‐ the scaling down of the programme in 1998;
‐ the payments made by the Commission to Greece in Autumn 1998 (final payment for 1997 and first advance payment for 1998); and
‐ an acceleration of implementation.

The current rates of payments and commitments amount to 38 % and 50 % respectively. The Commission shares the view that the take‐up rate will accelerate towards the end of the programming period.

The Monitoring Committee of the programme has just approved two large actions for the Integrated Lyceum consisting of extra tutorial support (ESF) and the establishment of laboratories (ERDF), the implementation of which will improve the take‐up rate of this measure. The 12 new university programmes were only adopted in October 1998 and it is too soon to meaningfully analyse the take‐up rate for these.

The Greek authorities have announced that the Community Support Framework will be reviewed in Spring 1999. The aim of this review is to ensure that all the necessary financial adjustments be made before the expiry date of 31 December 1999, in an effort to ensure full absorption of Community funds. The Commission believes that, one year before the expiration of the 2nd CSF, actions performing well should be strengthened.

The Commission considers that the creation of more tertiary education posts for students is in principle a positive development in the fight against unemployment. Employability can be improved by more effectively linking education systems to the needs of the labour market, strengthening post‐graduate studies in selected areas and promoting new technologies. The ESF supports such interventions.

Question No 94 by Jonas Sjöstedt (H−1128/98)

Subject: Compliance with EU directive on working hours

In a controversial case of social dumping, the Swedish company Ericsson has closed down production at Norrköping in Sweden to buy in the same components from sub‐contractors instead. One of the firms supplying the components in question to Ericsson is SCI Systems in Irvine, Scotland. SCI, Irvine, has no trade union organisation and, therefore, no collective agreement covering its employees. Wages are very low and a great many employees are working on short‐term contracts. The normal working week for many of them is 56 hours. Does the Commission consider that this situation is consistent with the EU directive on working hours? Does the Commission consider that the possibility of making exceptions for individual contracts is intended to be used systematically for all employees at an entire place of work? Does the Commission intend to make a closer investigation of conditions at SCI Systems in Irvine?

Answer

Article 18 of the Working Time Directive allows a Member State not to apply Article 6 of the Directive relating to an average working time of 48 hours per week under certain strict conditions. Furthermore, this Article states that before the expiry of a period of seven years from the date of the implementation of the Directive, the Council shall, on the basis of a Commission proposal accompanied by an appraisal report, re‐examine this option and decide on what action to take.

The United Kingdom has taken use of the option referred to above in its new Working Time Regulations, which came into force on 1 October 1998. The Commission is certainly of the view that it was not intended that this option be used systematically for all employees. However, it is only after a thorough analysis based on practical experiences with regard to the functioning of this option clause that the Commission can decide on the action to take. As the UK regulations have only very recently come into force, it would be premature to take a position on how this option is working in practice.

In any case, it is the responsibility of the authorities in the Member States to guarantee that the provisions of the Working Time Directive are respected. The Commission is undertaking a thorough analysis of the way in which the Directive has been implemented in the Member States with a view to presenting an implementation report during the course of 1999.

Question No 95 by Nikitas Kaklamanis (H−1131/98)

Subject: FIBA decision on basketball

The International Basketball Federation (FIBA) has decided to abolish the restriction on the number of non‐Community players in a team – currently a maximum of two – from the 1999‐2000 season.

Is the freedom of basketball players from third countries to establish themselves and work in Europe consistent with Community law?

How will the Commission respond to FIBA's decision, which sidelines European basketball players?

Was the above decision taken following talks with the European basketball players' union? If not, is such a procedure consistent with the EU's fundamental view that negotiations must take place before any decision is taken which affects working conditions?

Answer

Community law does not contradict FIBA"s decision to apply the principle of free movement to all basketball players.

The decision taken by FIBA would not appear to sideline European basketball players, rather it is aimed at recognising one particular fact: the globalisation of basketball. What is more, the decision does not contravene Community law regarding either the free movement of workers or free competition.

The FIBA decision was taken by its central committee, which includes representatives from national federations and brings together all those involved in the sport: clubs, trainers, players and umpires. Given that the decision was taken in line with its competences, it is incumbent upon FIBA to finalise the necessary contacts.

Question No 96 by John Cushnahan (H−1132/98)

Subject: Court of Auditors' report

Is the Commission aware that Paragraph 8.12 of the report adopted by the Court of Auditors on 23 September 1998(9) stated that: ‘the per capita GDP should not be the only criterion for judging the effectiveness of structural measures.'

In the light of this statement, would the Commission consider using alternative criteria, particularly GNP statistics?

Answer

The matter of how the effectiveness of Structural Fund measures is judged must be distinguished from questions relating to eligibility for regional objectives.

The special report by the Court of Auditors on the assessment of Structural Fund measures for the periods 1989 to 1993 and 1994 to 1999 stresses that the Commission should indicate the regional policy aspects which need to be more specifically assessed, such as additionality, convergence, partnership, concentration, employment, endogenous economic development or social welfare. Several criteria may be effectively used to assess the efficiency and effectiveness of the Structural Funds. For objective 1, the level of GDP per inhabitant is one indicator used among many. The Commission would point out that several thematic assessments have been launched on subjects such as support for SMUs, research and development, the environment, etc.

As far as eligibility for objective 1 is concerned, the criterion of GDP per inhabitant expressed in terms of purchasing power and calculated at the NUTS II level has been used since 1988 to determine the list of eligible regions. Regional gross domestic product is a composite indicator of production activity in a region. It is therefore well suited to the task of measuring the level of economic development of the regions. In accordance with Article 130a of the Treaty, which requires the Union to aim at reducing disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and the backwardness of the least favoured regions, Structural Fund measures under objective 1 are part of a regional context. However, data regarding gross domestic product, which measures the income of the resident population rather than the production generated, are not available at regional level.

In the case of further information on the NUTS II regions being required by Eurostat, or if the regions of Kerry and Clare are not suitable for inclusion in the NUTS II regions, will it be necessary for a new application to the submitted?

If Eurostat does not accept the proposal as it stands, it will be for the CSO to decide whether to submit an amended proposal.

Question No 98 by Hubert Pirker (H−1135/98)

Subject: Schools as drug‐free zones

On 12 May 1998 the European Parliament resolution on the control of new synthetic drugs (A4‐0157/98)(10) was adopted by a large majority.

There was broad support for my proposal that schools should voluntarily set themselves up as drug‐free zones as a preventive measure. Projects should be organised with school pupils, teachers, parents, school medical officers and drugs experts to draw attention to the dangers of taking drugs and develop defence mechanisms. In the concluding opinion on the report, that proposal was assessed very favourably.

The Commission was also asked to support this initiative and to submit a proposal setting out how schools might be helped to introduce this measure. Schools in Austria, Germany and France have already committed themselves to projects of this type and are awaiting support from the European Union.

What practical measures can the Commission suggest for assertively implementing the ‘schools are drug‐free zones' project as a preventive measure throughout the Union?

Answer

The Commission is providing support to health promoting schools in the Member States. It considers that school programmes are the core of primary prevention measures in all Member States; in this context, drug prevention is an educational priority which should be integrated in different aspects of school policy. The objectives of the preventive action at school are to give pupils the information and education for their personal development helping to raise their self‐esteem and problem solving skills in order to cope with conflict situations and peer‐pressure.

It is important to respect the diversity of preventive approaches developed in the various school environments, and also those which have been successful for the individual school.

Activities and/or actions for drug prevention in the school environment are eligible for support in the framework of the programme for Community action on the prevention of drug dependence (1996‐2000). In the 1999 work programme priority is given to schemes for advising teachers, families and those responsible for young people on the early detection of the use of drugs and the action to take.

The Commission has already received several applications for funding in respect of this action, some of which aim to implement preventive actions in the school environment.

The final selection of projects to be funded will be decided after the programme committee meets on 21 January 1999.

Question No 99 by Mark Killilea (H−1137/98)

Subject: Excise rules and duty‐free sales

The excise rules applicable after the abolition of duty‐free sales envisage reimbursement of excise duties when goods which are loaded on a ferry or aircraft in one country are then sold in a second country. How can this system function if excise duty has already been paid in the form of tax stamps in the first Member State?

Answer

According to Community legislation, economic operators have the possibility to claim reimbursement of excise duty with regard to excisable goods already released for consumption in one Member State and then sold in the territory of another Member State. However, as to the situation after the abolition of tax‐free sales, the Commission and the Member States envisage a system that makes such reimbursement procedures unnecessary. In order to avoid a second taxation, ferry operators or airlines can declare that they do not intend to sell goods within the territory of the second Member State. However in the unlikely case that they wish to continue sales of goods taxed in one Member State in the territory of a second Member State, they have to ensure the taxation of the goods in this second Member State. In the case of excisable goods bearing fiscal stamps, a practical solution would be the loading of two different stocks. As soon as the means of transport enters the territory of the second Member State the operators concerned would have to stop selling goods from the first stock and start to sell goods from the second stock bearing the fiscal stamps of this Member State. Since the goods already taxed in the first Member State are not offered for sale any more, there is no need to claim a reimbursement.

Question No 100 by Hans Lindqvist (H−1138/98)

Subject: Transport of animals within the EU

I am informed that there is an EU rule to the effect that all animals transported within the EU must be given the opportunity to lie down for rest and sleep. Does such a rule exist and, if so, does it apply to all animals or are there any exemptions, e.g. for horses?

Answer

Directive 91/628/EEC on the protection of animals during transport laid down the requirements concerning transport time, resting period, feeding and drinking.

In general domestic solipeds and domestic animals of the ovine, caprine, bovine and porcine species may not be transported longer than 8 hours.

However, when these species are transported in vehicles that meet a number of additional standards, then the animals can be transported during a longer period. The transport time will vary according to the species being transported.

After this maximum transport time, animals must be unloaded on a staging point, get food and water and they have to rest there for at least 24 hours. The standards for the staging points come into force on 1.1.1999.

Horses may be transported for 24 hours before they have to be unloaded on a staging point. During that 24 hours they must receive water at least each 8 hours and if necessary food as well.

The Council of Ministers requested the Commission to consider if a derogation could be made for breeding pigs, because of their special health status, and to allow transporters not to unload the pigs on a staging point.

The Commission has recently submitted a proposal to the Council on the possibility not to unload pigs at the staging point, but instead to leave them on the vehicle during that 24 hours rest period.

Question No 101 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H−1140/98)

Subject: Intentions regarding the negotiation of the next EU‐Morocco fisheries and cooperation agreement

In view of the fact that the current agreement expires next year and that action will have to be taken to anticipate and prevent loss or damage of any kind which may stem from the outcome of the negotiations, and also to avoid any excessive pressure from the time point of view, when is the Commission intending to initiate formally the negotiations on this major agreement which has for a long time linked our interests with those of Morocco?

Answer

The EU‐Morocco fisheries agreement entered into force on 1 December 1995 for a duration of four years. Consequently, as the honourable Member pointed out, it expires on 30 November 1999.

In order to guarantee continuity in relations with Morocco in terms of fisheries, the Commission intends to begin preliminary talks as soon as possible with the Member States most directly involved and with the fisheries sector.

In light of these talks, further discussions are planned with the Moroccan authorities in order to establish the framework for fisheries relations for the period after December 1999.

Question No 102 by Liam Hyland (H−1142/98)

Subject: Leader III

Under the LEADER II initiative, it is possible to fund the promotion and dissemination of innovation in rural areas. It also facilitates the exchange of experience and know‐how.

Does the Commission support the objective of a new LEADER initiative (III) and, if so, what new objectives does it consider should be introduced, particularly in relation to promoting further rural developments in Ireland?

Answer

The measures undertaken under LEADER I and LEADER II had beneficiary effects for the rural areas, but they have not given answers to all the problems to which the rural areas are and will be confronted. Consequently, for the next programming period, the Commission has proposed, within the framework of the Agenda 2000 package covering the structural funds, a Community Initiative devoted to rural development, in its widest sense.

The Community initiative for rural development will encourage local pilot strategies for the development of rural areas. These strategies will be based on the principles of bottom‐up approach and local partnership, so as to take full advantage of the potential existing in these areas. These strategies should respond in the most pertinent way to the various problems faced by the rural areas. In addition, successful solutions experienced by some areas will be disseminated all over Europe through important networking. In this context, Irish rural areas may benefit from this exchange of experiences.

Question No 103 by James Fitzsimons (H−1144/98)

Subject: Pollution from Sellafield

In late November, Greenpeace produced figures which indicated ‘worrying' levels of TC 99 from Sellafield in samples of seaweed taken from Cooley Point in Northern Ireland.

What reassurances can the Commission give to people living near Dundalk and Drogheda and the east coast of Ireland that this latest, in a long series of discoveries and incidents concerning Sellafield, is not ‘worrying', or does it now accept that this new discovery warrants the immediate closure of Sellafield and all related operations?

Answer

Technetium or TC 99 is a radionuclide of relatively low radiotoxicity which concentrates in particular in seaweed. Sellafield discharges of this nuclide have increased very significantly in recent years as a result of the processing of a backlog of liquid wastes which had been held in storage tanks. These wastes had been progressively consigned to storage tanks since the early 1980s when a decision was taken to design and construct a new effluent treatment plant for, in particular, the removal of alpha‐emitting nuclides. Treatment of the backlog began when the new plant was finally brought into operation but such treatment has little or no effect on technetium−99. Since then increased technetium concentrations in the environment have been widely detected, especially in seaweed which, as stated, exhibits greatly enhanced concentrations relative to those in other media.

The resulting radiation dose, however, even to the most highly exposed members of the population (via in particular the consumption of lobsters from the immediate vicinity of Sellafield), has contributed only a small fraction of the limit of exposure for members of the public. Nevertheless, reductions in the discharges of this nuclide have already been effected and the proposed revisions to the Sellafield discharge authorisations imply still further reductions.

Question No 104 by Niall Andrews (H−1148/98)

Subject: EU Youth and Older Persons' Cards

Can the Commission provide information on the availability of Youth and Older Persons' Cards in the EU Member States and the general concessions granted, and does it consider that common Youth and Older Persons' Cards should be introduced which would initially harmonise a common set of concessions such as travel and cultural events?

Answer

The Commission has launched two feasibility studies as a follow‐up to the Commission Recommendation of 10 May 1989 calling for the introduction of a Community‐wide over‐sixties' card. The first in 1993, covered the then 12 Member States, and one in 1995 looked at the situation in more detail in 5 Member States – Denmark, France, Ireland, the Netherlands and the UK. Copies of the final reports of these studies have been given to the Members of the European Parliament Intergroup on Ageing.

These reports show that, although all the Member States offer some form of concessionary scheme to older people, many do not use a card system, as such, and the conditions entitling people to transport and cultural concessions also vary greatly from one Member State to another. Several Member States have no national concession scheme but rely instead on a decentralised localised approach with variations between regions.

The number and variety of schemes operated by Member States, combined with the fact that the situation is constantly changing, make it difficult to have an accurate, up‐to‐date picture at European level.

The Commission remains convinced of the symbolic importance of a Community‐wide Older Persons' Card in promoting a stronger sense of European citizenship and in encouraging greater mutual acquaintance and understanding between citizens of the different Member States. The Commission is currently discussing the conclusions and recommendations for the second feasibility report with Member State representatives.

At present 12 Member States have a Youth Card for young people under the age of 26. 13 other non‐Community European countries have also developed a Youth Card.

With the support of the European Commission, the organisations running National Youth Cards set up and operate the European Youth Card Association (EYCA), a European non‐governmental organisation based in the Netherlands whose role is to coordinate Youth Cards throughout Europe and develop a common service‐oriented approach for the network.

In the countries where they operate, Youth Cards strive to provide holders with better and cheaper access to cultural, transport and other services; any youth card holder can benefit in a given country from the advantages offered by the Youth Card of that country.

In the context of the Youth for Europe programme – the main Community instrument for the promotion of cooperation in the youth field – the Commission has worked closely withe EYCA so that Youth Cards become a service‐oriented instrument that facilitates and encourages youth integration and mobility. In this context, facilitating access to information services for young people has also become a priority for the Youth Card network.

In the context of the development of the European Voluntary Service, a programme that offers young people the possibility to acquire a worthwhile vocational experience in another Member State while contributing to the wellbeing of the host community, the Commission is supporting EYCA's proposal to develop a separate European Youth Card for young volunteers.

Question No 105 by Per Gahrton (H−1155/98)

Subject: Schengen and the applicant countries

In the speech he delivered at the November 1998 part‐session in Strasbourg, the President of Poland, Alexander Kwasniewski, expressed his concern that the Schengen rules would make it difficult for people from Poland's eastern and southern neighbours, e.g. Ukraine, to visit Poland. He called for Poland to be allowed to take responsibility itself for maintaining ‘friendly' borders with neighbouring countries which have not joined the EU yet, or do not yet wish to join, or are still unable to join. Similarly, Hungary has warned that the Schengen rules may create new barriers between it and the large Hungarian minorities in countries such as Romania and Slovakia. There is also uneasiness in the Czech Republic about being forced to tighten up controls on its border with Slovakia, with which until not so long ago it had no border at all. There is concern in the Baltic States that it will be even harder to travel between them than it was during the Soviet era. Slovenia, too, is unhappy about having to introduce very tough border controls vis‐à‐vis other former Yugoslav republics.

What is the Commission's position as regards the concerns that exist in several applicant countries about being forced to erect new barriers on their borders with old neighbours who, for the time being at any rate, will remain outside the EU? Is it prepared for instance to grant derogations from the Schengen rules so as to allow new EU Member States to use their discretion in devising ways of maintaining ‘friendly' borders with neighbouring countries who will be remain outside the EU for the time being?

Answer

The Commission is aware of certain concerns which have been expressed in applicant States. The chapter Justice and Home Affairs will, in the first half of next year (1999), be the subject of screening with the countries which are negotiating for accession to the Union. The European Union's negotiating position on this chapter will subsequently be determined by the Member States, taking into account the negotiating positions to be presented by the applicant countries.

However, it is clear that discussions on Schengen will be based on Article 8 of the Schengen Protocol to the Amsterdam Treaty which states that for the purposes of the accession negotiations, the Schengen acquis shall be regarded as an acquis which must be accepted in full by all candidate States for admission.

Question No 106 by Vassilis Ephremidis (H−1159/98)

Subject: Subsidisation of project to build ‘ancillary premises' to Athens Concert Hall

The award of the contract to design and build the ‘ancillary premises' to the Athens Concert Hall has once again highlighted the issue of funding for this project. Although the entire project is being paid for from Greek public funds and Community subsidies, it is to be owned, designed, built, used and managed by a private concern. Despite protests from local residents and numerous cultural organisations about the adverse environmental impact, the EU has approved subsidies for 50 % of the cost of the project from the ERDF and the regional operational programme for Attica.

Following the announcement that construction of the underground opera‐house – referred to as Congress Centre in Community programmes and cultural centre in the Greek ministerial decrees – will cost Drs 71 billion instead of the estimated GRD 17 bn, will the 50 % subsidy for the project be maintained? If so, from where will the requisite appropriations be deducted and, if not, what will the maximum subsidy be and what sources will provide the rest of the funds required?

Answer

The Commission confirms that it is to cofinance the work on the Athens Concert Hall through the ‘Tourism‐Culture" programme and the Attica programme, on the basis of a total cost of EUR 50.7 million (GRD 15 billion).

As a result, the Commission cannot at present confirm the figure of ECU 212 million (GRD 71 billion) that the honourable Member mentioned. It will ask the Greek authorities for more information on this and will pass it on to you accordingly.

Question No 107 by Karin Riis‐Jørgensen (H−1160/98)

Subject: Ban on sales of ‘De blå svin' energy drink in Denmark

In 1998, the Danish government has refused to allow certain energy drinks with added vitamins and caffeine to be imported and sold. It has done so despite the fact that such energy drinks have been approved and marketed in 13 other Member States. The Danish government is adamant that the people of Denmark are not undernourished and will therefore not approve the drinks, as it is feared that people's health will suffer as a result of an uncontrolled intake of vitamins.

The vitamins in the drinks are all water‐soluble, and any excess intake will naturally be excreted. In other words, they will not accumulate in the body and cause damage, and it cannot be proven that this product poses a specific risk.

Does the Commission consider that the ban on sales of this energy drink runs counter to the principle of the free movement of goods and hence constitutes a technical barrier to trade? If so, what action will it take to remove that barrier?

Answer

Danish prohibition regarding the ‘De blå svin' energy drink has not come to the Commission's knowledge. However, the Commission is generally well aware of problems in certain Member States relating to bans on other so‐called energy drinks and to prohibitions on adding vitamins and minerals to these drinks.

Indeed, the Commission has received a number of complaints from economic operators unable to import and market these drinks, for instance Red Bull, in Denmark. The Commission has accordingly initiated infringement proceedings against Denmark calling into question the compatibility of the Danish ban with Articles 30‐36 of the EC Treaty.

The Commission is currently examining the arguments advanced by the Danish authorities in support of their position.

In the absence of harmonised Community rules, the Member States have the right to adopt and enforce national regulations provided that these are in conformity with the provisions on the free movement of goods. More particularly, as regards bans on foodstuffs or drinks which have been legally manufactured and or marketed in another Member States, these can be upheld only if they are strictly necessary for the protection of public health. It is for the importing Member State to prove that to be the case.

Question No 108 by Anne Van Lancker (H−1161/98)

Subject: New discrimination against frontier workers – Netherlands‐Belgian double taxation agreement discriminates against Dutch frontier workers

The new Netherlands‐Belgian draft double taxation agreement provides for the country‐of‐ employment principle for the taxation of frontier workers. In its resolution of 28.5.1998 on the situation of frontier workers the European Parliament advocated such solutions. However, the introduction of this provision without transitional arrangements will cause problems for Dutch frontier workers in Belgium who will lose about NGL 6000 each year in purchasing power. On the other hand, the recent changes in the collection of national insurance contributions and the reduction of taxation under the Netherlands PEMBA (Invalidity Insurance Act) legislation has caused considerable loss of income for Belgian frontier workers. In neither of these cases did the authorities concerned carry out a Europe test to check on the consequences of the changes in legislation for frontier workers. In its resolution the European Parliament calls for a Commission initiative for the introduction of a compulsory Europe test prior to all changes to social and fiscal legislation. Commissioner Flynn responded a somewhat cautiously, arguing that the existing Commission instruments were adequate and that the Commission would encourage discussion between the authorities concerned. It is quite clear that it is not enough to rely on the goodwill of the Member States. Is the Commission now prepared to take action on a proposal for a Europe test?

Answer

Belgium and the Netherlands are indeed in the process of negotiating a new tax agreement. However, negotiations have not yet ended and this new agreement, bearing in mind the ratification procedures, will only enter into force in a few years" time.

According to the information the Commission has available, it appears that current negotiations, in terms of the provisions of the new agreement, seem to want to provide for the taxing of frontier workers in the country where they work instead of the country of origin as is current practice.

Given that Belgian income tax seems to be higher than Dutch income tax, this would – as the honourable Member pointed out – lead to a heavier tax burden on Dutch frontier workers working in Belgium.

In this connection, the Commission notes that income tax and fiscal agreements that govern the right of taxation in relations between Member States come under the competence of the Member States themselves. The Commission is of the opinion that the Member States are currently in the best position to decide between themselves on how to distribute the right of taxation, which naturally has considerable repercussions on countries" tax receipts and on the fiscal situation of those involved.

The Commission favours negotiations between Member States in relation to changes to the legislation in question, which affects frontier workers. However, for the time being, it does not intend to put forward any proposals in this area.

Question No 109 by Hugh McMahon (H−1163/98)

Subject: Commission Communication on Ageing

Can the Commission inform Parliament when it will publish its Communication on Ageing and whether it will appear in time to promote EU actions to celebrate the 1999 International Year of Older People?

Answer

The Commission intends to adopt a Communication on Ageing in January 1999. The Communication will constitute the contribution of the Commission to the UN International Year of Older Persons, and give an overview of Community activities relevant to the themes of the year.

Question No 110 by Gianni Tamino (H−1164/98)

Subject: Golf course in Northern Greece

The wetlands of Stavronicita, in Northern Greece, consist of a complex of two marshes covering approximately 300 hectares. These wetlands are characterised by an important number of protected (under Directive 79/409/EEC)(11) species (mainly avian and amphibian) as well as an important range of flora. The Stavronicita wetlands are now threatened by the construction of a golf course as part of the tourist development of this part of Northern Greece.

As the European Parliament (in particular the Green Group) has always stressed the importance of environmentally sound and sustainable tourism, how is the Commission reconciling tourist development with environmental protection in this case, where a large company is planning to destroy an area which is important for a large variety of species in order to create a golf course?

Does the European Commission know about this golf course project and does it intend to influence in some way the Greek Ministry for Development to re‐evaluate and possibly stop the project?

Answer

The Commission became aware of the information provided by the honourable Member following a complaint it received. In fact, the site in question does not feature among the special protection areas (SPA) as defined in the Birds Directive 79/409/EEC. Nor has it been proposed by Greece as a site of Community importance, in accordance with the Habitats Directive 92/43/EEC(12) . What is more, it is not included in the respective scientific reference lists, namely the special bird conservation areas (SAC) and sites eligible for the Natura 2000 network under the Habitats Directive. Nonetheless, the ecological importance of the two marshes on the site is highlighted in an inventory of Greek wetlands published in 1994 by the Greek Centre for Biotopes/Wetlands (EKBY). And according to the Commission"s information, the site will be included in the revised SAC list to be published shortly.

The Commission is in the process of dealing with the abovementioned complaint on the basis of information it has received that demonstrates the importance of the site for nature conservation. Within this context, on 23 January 1998, the Commission contacted the Greek authorities in order to confirm that the environmental impact assessment of the project in question (as provided for by current Greek legislation) would include sufficient coverage of the ecological value of this wetland. In reply to this letter, the Greek authorities informed the Commission, by letter dated 7 July 1998, that the project had been temporarily suspended, due, in particular, to gaps in the impact assessment. They referred, moreover, to the decision taken by the Greek Council of State on 5 March 1998, which temporarily suspends the decision by the Greek Tourist Board (EOT) to give this site to a company hoping to turn it into a golf course.

As a general rule, in accordance with Article 6 of Directive 92/43, any plan or project likely to have a significant effect on a Natura 2000 site shall be subject to appropriate assessment of its implications for the site to ensure that the site"s integrity and overall coherence of Natura 2000 is protected.

Question No 111 by Elly Plooij‐van Gorsel (H−1165/98)

Subject: Compliance with European public tendering rules by the Netherlands Broadcasting Production Company

On 3 March 1998 I tabled an oral question (H−0290/98) for Question Time on compliance with European tendering rules by the Netherlands Broadcasting Production Company (NOB)(13) . The Commission's reply indicated that the NOB must comply with the European public tendering rules only if it is a contracting authority and can be classified as a public corporation. As it was not clear to the Commission at that stage whether the NOB fulfilled all the conditions to be classified as a public corporation, it indicated that it would make inquiries on this matter.

Can the Commission inform me of the results of this inquiry?

On the basis of its findings, can the Commission indicate whether the NOB is obliged to comply with the European public tendering rules?

Answer

Following the oral question (H−0290/98) tabled by the honourable Member, the Commission made inquiries to find out whether or not the Nederlandse Omroepproduktie Bedrijf (the Dutch broadcasting production company) should be subject to European rules on public procurement. It had to ascertain whether or not this company should be classed as a ‘contracting authority" and more specifically as a ‘body governed by public law" as defined by Community directives on ‘public procurement".

By ‘body governed by public law" we understand any body:

‐ created specifically to meet needs in the general interest other than industrial or commercial, and

‐ that has the legal personnel required, and

‐ whose activities are either chiefly financed by the Member State, government authorities or other bodies governed by public law, whose management is subject to their control, or whose board of directors, administrative or monitoring body comprises members of whom over half have been appointed by the State, local government or other bodies governed by public law.
These three conditions are cumulative: if one of the conditions is not fulfilled, the body in question cannot be classed as a ‘body governed by public law".

On the basis of the available information, the Commission notes that these three conditions have, in fact, been fulfilled and that, therefore, the Nederlandse Omroepproduktie Bedrijf should be classed as a body governed by public law. As a result, it should be subject to the rules of public procurement directives.

In order to ensure that the public procurement directives are complied with, the Commission will undertake negotiations with the Member State concerned. It will draw its attention to the Nederlandse Omroepproduktie Bedrijf "s obligation to respect these directives and will ask the Member State to provide guarantees concerning the respect of these obligations in the future.

Question No 112 by Inger Schörling (H−1167/98)

Subject: Sweden's accession treaty

At the end of 1998, a number of the derogations Sweden secured in its pre‐accession negotiations with the EU will expire. The intention was said to be that the Commission, during the four‐year transitional period, would consider each of the derogations with a view to applying the relevant Swedish provisions throughout the Union. This has happened in some cases, but consideration of several derogations will not have been completed by the end of the year. Sweden will therefore find itself in a difficult situation: should it disregard the fact that the derogations have expired and hope for good news from the Commission, or should it bring its national rules into line with EU rules?

Does the Commission take the view that the terms of Sweden's accession treaty permit derogations to be maintained after the end of 1998 in cases where it has not managed to reach an agreement with Sweden? What action will it take if it considers that such derogations cannot be maintained under the terms of the accession treaty?

Answer

In almost all cases of the Review Process, solutions have been found within the time period in order to enable Sweden to keep their high environmental and health standards after the expiry of the derogations in the Act of Accession. In many cases, Community legislation has already been adopted which strengthen the standards all over the Community. In some cases the Commission has tabled the necessary proposals under the different directives within the scheduled time and they now remain with the European Parliament and the Council for decision making. In other situations, the relevant committee procedures have been triggered by the Commission and they are expected to be finalised as soon as possible.

In a limited number of cases time gaps between the end of the transitional period and the final adoption of new acts might occur. Nevertheless, no practical problems are expected since all parties concerned are aware of ongoing developments. The legal changes will either correspond to the Swedish standards for the protection of human health and the environment or enable them to have a prolonged time period for the derogations in question.

Question No 113 by Antonios Trakatellis (H−1171/98/rev. 1)

Subject: Compliance with environment law and transmission of electric power via overhead cables

There were serious clashes between police and local residents on 3 December at Krioneri, Attica, when the Greek Electricity Board (DEI) attempted to erect pylons and high‐tension electricity cables. The local community has appealed to the Council of State to enforce the provisions of Directives 85/337/EEC(14) and 97/11/EEC(15) , which have been contravened by DEI's decisions.

Are DEI's pylons and high‐tension cables a threat to public health and the local community or not?

Is there or is there not a risk from high‐tension cables passing through residential areas such as Krioneri and why does DEI not run the cables outside the populated area as it could?

Have the directives on environmental impact assessment been breached in this particular case and, if so, what measures will the Commission take to enforce Community law?

Since the EIA should be drawn up by experts, what is the Commission's view of the assessment submitted by DEI?

Answer

Within the framework of monitoring the implementation of Community environmental law in Greece, the Commission attaches the utmost importance to any complaint or other denunciation made concerning projects carried out by the Greek Electricity Board (DEI) that could have a negative impact on the environment. In this context, information has been requested from the national authorities concerning the environmental impact assessment carried out in accordance with Directive 85/337/EEC(16) . The Commission is examining the content of these assessments before deciding on how to follow up these cases.

The Commission is aware of the DEI"s project for the transmission of electric power via overhead cables at Krioneri, in Attica, a project whose completion was previously suspended following a decision taken by the Greek Council of State. The environmental impact assessment that has since been carried out received ministerial approval and the DEI has recently started work again in an attempt to complete the project.

Since the project does not receive Community funding, the Commission does not, at present, have all the information it needs on the matter. It is therefore not in a position to answer the honourable Member"s question regarding the project"s impact on the region"s environment.

Taking into account the information provided by the honourable Member concerning the demonstrations on 3 December 1998 against the project, the Commission will contact the Greek authorities to gain more information regarding the project"s scope and the latest impact assessment carried out.

As regards the issue of the impact of electric cables on health, the Commission can confirm the following.

Through the fourth and fifth framework programmes on research, the Commission has supported, and continues to support, a large number of studies on induced electromagnetic fields (EMFs), particularly through high‐tension electricity cables. There is no definitive answer to the question of the impact of EMFs on health. However, according to current scientific research, there is no convincing scientific evidence to suggest that EMFs have long‐term effects on health. The acute effects of EMF exposure are well‐known and are relatively easily controlled. What is more, a recommendation was put forward by the Commission in June on limiting the public"s exposure to electromagnetic fields(17) . This proposal for a recommendation is currently being discussed by the Council.
